                                 will meet
                         7 PM Thursday Jul 3, 2008

             College of Engineering & Computer Science Building
                              Room FAB 86-01
                       (This is in the basement.)
          The building is on SW 4th across from SW College Street.
       See location H-10 on map at http://pdxLinux.org/campus_map.jpg

    *******************************************************************

                                PRESENTATION

                         Stupid USB Keyboard Tricks
          Reading magstripes, barcodes, 10-key pads, and keyboards
                                    with
                                  libusb.

                                     by

                                Eric Wilhelm
                        <scratchcomputing at gmail.com>

    An overview of libusb, how usb keyboards (and keyboard-like
    devices) are handled by Linux and X, and how they can be
    disconnected from the main input driver for use and abuse
    by user-space code.

    This technology has useful applications such as connecting
    USB credit-card and barcode readers to point-of-sale software
    independently from the keyboard focus or keymap.  It also allows
    silly things like multi-player text editors or a 105-command
    program launcher.  Or you could just use it to change songs in xmms.

    Code will shown in small doses of Perl and C.  Hands-on
    demonstrations will be included as time allows.

    Caution:  presentation may result in a desire to attach one
    or more additional devices to your computer.

    *******************************************************************

From bthoen at gisnet.com  Tue Jul  1 07:52:05 2008
From: bthoen at gisnet.com (Bill Thoen)
Date: Tue, 01 Jul 2008 08:52:05 -0600
Subject: [PLUG] Can iptables Block a Range of IPs?
In-Reply-To: <1214842923.5633.4.camel@localhost.localdomain>
References: <20080628133835.GA20469@www.gisnet.com>	<alpine.OSX.1.10.0806281336370.819@guild.galois.com>	<4867F04D.1030004@gisnet.com>	<20080629210914.GA15909@pickled.herring.org>
	<1214842923.5633.4.camel@localhost.localdomain>
Message-ID: <486A4495.2080009@gisnet.com>

Thanks for all the help, everyone.

However, as I was going through the process of getting a copy of 
denyhosts, I had a different idea that I think is better. Rather than 
chase after these zombies trying to brute-force their way into my 
machine, I thought, why not just change the default sshd port? Now they 
can bang away on port 22 and get nowhere, and I don't have t look at 
their attempts in my logs and get annoyed.

But there's a new twist in FC 9 if it's running SELinux. Besides 
changing the SSH port at your firewall and in /etc/ssh/sshd_config, you 
also have to tell SELinux to add your new ssh port to its ssh_port_t 
label. To do this, (if you  used 1234 as your new ssh port) the command is:

||/usr/sbin/semanage port -a -t ssh_port_t -p tcp 1234

There's a pretty decent web page that describes this and other SELinux 
isssues at http://equivocation.org/node/124.

- Bill Thoen

> Tom> could install on our embedded PC that would let us monitor (and
> Tom> store for later analysis) these HTML strings / data packets in a
> Tom> user-friendly and human-readable form?
>
> Capture whole packets with tcpdump[1], load the result into wireshark[2] 
> and
> filter away the traffic you don't care about.
>
> [1] tcpdump -i <iface> -s 1500 -w /tmp/capture.pcap
> [2] wireshark capture.pcap

The less help ReactOS gets, the longer it is going to take to stabilize
it.  It would be awesome if the team could stabilize it early.  Aside
from Dirk Dashing Secret Agent, I don't see much effort to design
popular software for Linux.  The majority of effort, even by my game
company, is to produce software for Windows.  This doesn't just hurt
Linux adoption, it hurts adoption of other OS'es as well.  This Windows
bias that Intuit and other software companies are guilty of will give
Microsoft a stranglehold on the software market until an OS like ReactOS
takes off or wine gets 1000 times better.  Wine and ReactOS are
developing together, so helping the Wine project helps ReactOS.

     Michael Robinson

haven't used them before.)

There are only a few reviews on newegg but I got the impress the R280 
goes through ink fast and the Epson cartridges (one manufacturer is in 
China and the other is in Mexico) may not play well with each other. 
Make me worry about compatible cartridges.

I looked at a couple of HP Photosmart but they don't do CD/DVD.

So anyone using a Epson R280?

Anyone used carrotink before?

Subject: [PLUG] Linksys w/ dd-wrt suddenly goes south
Message-ID: <486BEFE2.2000304@acm.org>

I have a Linksys WRT54GL running dd-wrt v23 standard that has suddenly
started going south at random times (as in several times a day).  It is
on a LAN with a Win2k server providing Active Directory services.
Previously the LAN used route-able IPs and now is on a private IP range. 
  There is most certainly residue of the old LAN still on the server.

When the Linksys goes down I can still get a wireless connection but my
N800 reports it as a link-local connection.  Typically I've seen this
when the DHCP lease does not include gateway information.

Currently I'm thinking it is flaky hardware but I'm looking for other
ideas.  There is a replacement Linksys coming, hopefully today, but I'm
hedging my bets in case it is something missed during the LAN changeover
and now a Windows service (DHCP, DNS) has come active and is using the
old network settings.

Any ideas?


TIA,
Rod

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Thu Jul  3 00:23:08 2008
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Thu, 3 Jul 2008 00:23:08 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Linksys w/ dd-wrt suddenly goes south
In-Reply-To: <486BEFE2.2000304@acm.org>
References: <486BEFE2.2000304@acm.org>
Message-ID: <20080703072308.GB9587@gate.kl-ic.com>

On Wed, Jul 02, 2008 at 02:15:14PM -0700, Roderick A. Anderson wrote:
> I have a Linksys WRT54GL running dd-wrt v23 standard that has suddenly
> started going south at random times (as in several times a day).  It is
> on a LAN with a Win2k server providing Active Directory services.
> Previously the LAN used route-able IPs and now is on a private IP range. 
>  There is most certainly residue of the old LAN still on the server.
> 
> When the Linksys goes down I can still get a wireless connection but my
> N800 reports it as a link-local connection.  Typically I've seen this
> when the DHCP lease does not include gateway information.
> 
> Currently I'm thinking it is flaky hardware but I'm looking for other
> ideas.  There is a replacement Linksys coming, hopefully today, but I'm
> hedging my bets in case it is something missed during the LAN changeover
> and now a Windows service (DHCP, DNS) has come active and is using the
> old network settings.

If the traffic is increasing, or the load on the wall power circuit
is increasing (an air conditioner is attached, sagging the line power)
or the WRT is in a hotter room or any number of other environmental
conditions apply, then it may not be a flaky WRT. Instead, it can be
a flaky wall wart power supply.  Many of these seem to be marginal,
and in stressful conditions they can crap out, causing resets.  Increased
traffic load means increased packet power, which increases the load
on the wall wart.

Note that you can power a WRT with many 16V IBM Thinkpad laptop
adapters.  See the somewhat obsolete writeup at:

    http://www.keithl.com/Linksyspower.html

Note that since the sale to Lenovo, some new Thinkpads use a different,
larger diameter barrel on the AC adapter.  So check the connector size,
and voltage, first before ordering.  I know that the AC adapters for
the THinkpad 560, 570, T20, and T30 will work, and probably others,
and you can find these cheap on Ebay.

Also, just try cleaning the connector on the output of the wall wart.
If it is corroded, you may be losing power right there.

Keith

-- 
Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993
KLIC --- Keith Lofstrom Integrated Circuits --- "Your Ideas in Silicon"
Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

                                PRESENTATION

                         Stupid USB Keyboard Tricks
          Reading magstripes, barcodes, 10-key pads, and keyboards
                                    with
                                  libusb.

                                     by

                                Eric Wilhelm
                        <scratchcomputing at gmail.com>

    An overview of libusb, how usb keyboards (and keyboard-like
    devices) are handled by Linux and X, and how they can be
    disconnected from the main input driver for use and abuse
    by user-space code.

    This technology has useful applications such as connecting
    USB credit-card and barcode readers to point-of-sale software
    independently from the keyboard focus or keymap.  It also allows
    silly things like multi-player text editors or a 105-command
    program launcher.  Or you could just use it to change songs in xmms.

    Code will shown in small doses of Perl and C.  Hands-on
    demonstrations will be included as time allows.

    or more additional devices to your computer.

From gently at gmail.com  Thu Jul  3 09:34:12 2008
From: gently at gmail.com (chris (fool) mccraw)
Date: Thu, 3 Jul 2008 09:34:12 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] ssh problem...
In-Reply-To: <1215045714.5259.0.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>
References: <1215045714.5259.0.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>
Message-ID: <ae2db4620807030934l54e122eao330c56dbc61eaf78@mail.gmail.com>

On 7/2/08, robinson-west user <plug_1 at robinson-west.com> wrote:

>     Another symptom of the problem is that an ssh session to this
>  machine will often times lock up when I run a command like cat on a
>  large file.  The wired equivalent that would cause this would be
>  unplugging the network line.  Tried pinging to test the link,
>  0% packet loss even if I ping 200 times.  Well, ls can lock the
>  session up also.  In a couple of days, I'll up the onboard ram
>  by a factor of 4.
>
>      I guess the question is, how do I get more information about
>  this problem?

same way you debug any other problem of unknown origin and nature:
try to isolate the failure case.

since you have something that will trigger it, try seeing if you can
find the exact length of output that will trigger it every time, and
if removing one byte will always save you--is the problem consistent?
try making the file you cat contain different characters--are all
spaces ok?  maybe it's a control character that hangs up the
connection.  does using ssh with -v to connect give you any more
information during the disconnection?  do any other connection types
(telnet, web) also hang?

this is sounding to me more and more like corrupted network packets,
which could come from bad cabling, cards, ram, switch (if any), cable
location (someone once told me that running net cables alongside power
cables would lead to interference).  does moving the machines to be
physically adjacent, directly corrected with a different cable, help
at all?

i'd also fire up tcpdump and see if there's anything obvious about the
failing packets--especially if you can reproduce the failure exactly
with something that's not ssh so you can read the plaintext contents
and see if any bits are flipped.

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Thu Jul  3 10:29:17 2008
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Thu, 3 Jul 2008 10:29:17 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Linksys w/ dd-wrt suddenly goes south

> is increasing (an air conditioner is attached, sagging the line power)
> or the WRT is in a hotter room or any number of other environmental
> conditions apply, then it may not be a flaky WRT. Instead, it can be

On Thu, Jul 03, 2008 at 06:13:52AM -0700, Roderick A. Anderson wrote:

> I didn't think of this and I am using the same socket that the Sonicwall 
> was using.  Could be a marginal wall-wart.  It has been pretty hot here 
> during the day.  The flip side to that is all the comm equipment is here 
> and it is on a UPS so hopefully I'm not getting caught by the 
> side-splash of an infinite-improbability-drive.


Keith responds:

I don't think I made myself clear.  This is not a "digital" thing.
It has more to do with a summation of small problems putting your
Linksys over the edge on apparently random occasions.

Your Linksys was designed for a home environment, working straight
out of the box and through some short warranty period, on average,
without too many resets.  Let's say that the average Linksys has 
a "power safety margin" of 1.5 under these conditions.  If the
margin drops below 1.0, it may fail.  The margin reducers rarely
line up, but they do from time to time.


1) Unless you have an "offline" UPS, it only makes things worse. 
An offline UPS converts wall AC power to battery voltage to output
AC power, and removing wall power has no immediate effect on the
output AC power.  If you need steady power, you must have one of
these.

The vast majority of storebought UPSs are the cheaper "online"
variety - the output is connected to wall power until it fails,
and then a relay switches the output from wall power to battery
generated power when the wall voltage drops below a threshold
(and switches again when the wall power comes back).

During the transition, as the relay contacts are moving from one
source to another, the output voltage drops to ZERO;  usually
for one or two line power cycles.  Most monitors and CPU power
supplies have enough internal energy storage to keep running through
the zero voltage time.  Not so the Linksys - its internal energy
storage is trivial, just enough to keep running between the lobes
of a robust power sine wave when drawing maximum power.  The Linksys
will do a power-on reset if power is gone for a very brief time.  


2) If you are transmitting more packets than a typical home setup
( back in the era the Linksys was designed for, 1Mbit DSL ), then
Linksys sucks down more power than it was designed for.  It needs
very steady power to keep running in these conditions.  It will
not get it under condition (1).


3) The Linksys draws 6 Watts transmitting.  The wall wart provides 1
amp, on average, approximately.  On average, the wall wart provides
that at 12V, but looking at the waveform with an oscilloscope, under
ideal conditions, the voltage bounces around between 8V and 16V
with an 8.3 millisecond period.  Power is current times voltage,
so 1A times 8V is 8W, only a small margin over 6W needed.  If the
margin goes negative, the Linksys loses power and resets.


4) The voltage waveform from your wall socket is not ideal.  If a
heavy load goes on line, like an industrial motor or a refridgerator
or an air conditioning pump anywhere on your branch circuit, it will
"sag" the power line voltage, possibly tripping the UPS, or else
just passing it through.  This can push the margin negative.  There
are voltage regulators upstream of your wall socket, but they take
some time to respond to loads.  In fact, the load of a shaded pole
induction motor at startup looks pretty close to a dead short.


5) The power connectors from the wall wart to the Linksys are
pretty crappy.  If you want a good, low resistance power connection
between units, you want gold-plated connectors.  If you don't have
that, then the connectors can corrode over time, increasing their
resistance.  Of course, that doesn't affect the unit new out of
the store (Linksys as a 100 foot, 100 second warranty as near as
I can tell).  So if corrosion over time causes half an ohm of
resistance in the connection (typically on the inner connection
pin, but also on the barrel) then that will take away about half
a volt of the supplied power under low voltage conditions.  You
can clean the connectors to improve this, although you should not
do this frequently or it will take off the chrome plating.


6) Miscellaneous other margin-reducing events.  For example, the
original WRT was flashed in the factory, for the first time, under
near-ideal conditions.  Flashing it a second or third time under
non-ideal conditions will make a few of the NVRAM bits a little
"weaker" and sensitive to smaller margins.  Some instructions on
a processor require more power than others.  Some patterns of
signals on interconnect wires require more power than others.
There are lots of little effects like this, some in hardware, some
in software.  Aging plays a big role in many of these effects.  


It is the sum of all these effects that can push a Linksys into the
negative margin regime.  With intermittent results;  if the transmit
packets occur during a line voltage sine wave peak, there is plenty
of margin.   If a burst of transmit packets occur while the AC cycle
is crossing zero, after a motor-induced power sag, on a hot day when
resistance is high, over somewhat corroded connectors, and you go
into negative margin and reset your Linksys.  Or your Actiontec, or
whatever cheap unit you've got.

Why does a laptop AC adaptor improve this scenario?  Primarily, it is
because the adapters have a large energy bucket inside, and are designed
to produce a regulated DC output voltage under a wider range of power
line conditions, to a heavier load.  They can produce 40 Watts, not 6W,
and they can provide it over many missing power line cycles.  Think of
it as a minature offline UPS.  If you use a 16V adapter, the Linksys
internal regulator draws 6W/16V or about 0.4 amps during transmitting,
and this means less power loss due to corrosion.   Empirically, using
16V laptop AC adaptors for powering Linksys devices JUST WORKS, with
almost no unexplained resets compared to the marginally inadequate
wall warts.  Again, read the web page I mention above.


Computer people often have a hard time understanding soft margins. 
It is assumed that something is either good or bad, without a gray
area.  That is NOT how things work in the real world.  I promised
to give a lecture to PLUG, "bits are a myth" (Real Soon Now), where
I explain the analog nature of the "Real World" and why the digital
approximation often fails.  Robust systems are designed with this
in mind.  Robust software is designed to sense marginal conditions
and recover quickly from small nondeterministic behavioral upsets.
That means NEVER assuming that a sequence of correct instructions
always produces the same result.  Always, always, test the result!

As we enter the era of unsteady power supply, and gigantic high speed
systems pushed right to the margin of failure, system designers and
programmers must take these effects into account or watch their
products exhibit "mysterious" "flaky" behavior in the field.  There
is actually nothing mysterious about the behavior; it is a fact of
life.   The mystery is why programmers cling to the myth of complete
mechanical determinism when experience contradicts it so often.

Keith

-- 
Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993
KLIC --- Keith Lofstrom Integrated Circuits --- "Your Ideas in Silicon"
Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

>> or the WRT is in a hotter room or any number of other environmental
>> conditions apply, then it may not be a flaky WRT. Instead, it can be
>> a flaky wall wart power supply.  Many of these seem to be marginal,
>> and in stressful conditions they can crap out, causing resets.  Increased
>> traffic load means increased packet power, which increases the load
>> on the wall wart.

>> larger diameter barrel on the AC adapter.  So check the connector size,

>> the THinkpad 560, 570, T20, and T30 will work, and probably others,
>> and you can find these cheap on Ebay.
>>
>> Also, just try cleaning the connector on the output of the wall wart.
>> If it is corroded, you may be losing power right there.

-- 
> 
> Your Linksys was designed for a home environment, working straight
> out of the box and through some short warranty period, on average,
> without too many resets.  Let's say that the average Linksys has 
> a "power safety margin" of 1.5 under these conditions.  If the
> margin drops below 1.0, it may fail.  The margin reducers rarely
> line up, but they do from time to time.
> 
> 
> 1) Unless you have an "offline" UPS, it only makes things worse. 
> An offline UPS converts wall AC power to battery voltage to output
> AC power, and removing wall power has no immediate effect on the
> output AC power.  If you need steady power, you must have one of
> these.
> 
> The vast majority of storebought UPSs are the cheaper "online"
> variety - the output is connected to wall power until it fails,
> and then a relay switches the output from wall power to battery
> generated power when the wall voltage drops below a threshold
> (and switches again when the wall power comes back).
> 
> During the transition, as the relay contacts are moving from one
> source to another, the output voltage drops to ZERO;  usually
> for one or two line power cycles.  Most monitors and CPU power
> supplies have enough internal energy storage to keep running through
> the zero voltage time.  Not so the Linksys - its internal energy
> storage is trivial, just enough to keep running between the lobes
> of a robust power sine wave when drawing maximum power.  The Linksys
> will do a power-on reset if power is gone for a very brief time.  
> 
> 
> 2) If you are transmitting more packets than a typical home setup
> ( back in the era the Linksys was designed for, 1Mbit DSL ), then
> Linksys sucks down more power than it was designed for.  It needs
> very steady power to keep running in these conditions.  It will
> not get it under condition (1).
> 
> 
> 3) The Linksys draws 6 Watts transmitting.  The wall wart provides 1
> amp, on average, approximately.  On average, the wall wart provides
> that at 12V, but looking at the waveform with an oscilloscope, under
> ideal conditions, the voltage bounces around between 8V and 16V
> with an 8.3 millisecond period.  Power is current times voltage,
> so 1A times 8V is 8W, only a small margin over 6W needed.  If the
> margin goes negative, the Linksys loses power and resets.
> 
> 
> 4) The voltage waveform from your wall socket is not ideal.  If a
> heavy load goes on line, like an industrial motor or a refridgerator
> or an air conditioning pump anywhere on your branch circuit, it will
> "sag" the power line voltage, possibly tripping the UPS, or else
> just passing it through.  This can push the margin negative.  There
> are voltage regulators upstream of your wall socket, but they take
> some time to respond to loads.  In fact, the load of a shaded pole
> induction motor at startup looks pretty close to a dead short.
> 
> 
> 5) The power connectors from the wall wart to the Linksys are
> pretty crappy.  If you want a good, low resistance power connection
> between units, you want gold-plated connectors.  If you don't have
> that, then the connectors can corrode over time, increasing their
> resistance.  Of course, that doesn't affect the unit new out of
> the store (Linksys as a 100 foot, 100 second warranty as near as
> I can tell).  So if corrosion over time causes half an ohm of
> resistance in the connection (typically on the inner connection
> pin, but also on the barrel) then that will take away about half
> a volt of the supplied power under low voltage conditions.  You
> can clean the connectors to improve this, although you should not
> do this frequently or it will take off the chrome plating.
> 
> 
> 6) Miscellaneous other margin-reducing events.  For example, the
> original WRT was flashed in the factory, for the first time, under
> near-ideal conditions.  Flashing it a second or third time under
> non-ideal conditions will make a few of the NVRAM bits a little
> "weaker" and sensitive to smaller margins.  Some instructions on
> a processor require more power than others.  Some patterns of
> signals on interconnect wires require more power than others.
> There are lots of little effects like this, some in hardware, some
> in software.  Aging plays a big role in many of these effects.  
> 
> 
> It is the sum of all these effects that can push a Linksys into the
> negative margin regime.  With intermittent results;  if the transmit
> packets occur during a line voltage sine wave peak, there is plenty
> of margin.   If a burst of transmit packets occur while the AC cycle
> is crossing zero, after a motor-induced power sag, on a hot day when
> resistance is high, over somewhat corroded connectors, and you go
> into negative margin and reset your Linksys.  Or your Actiontec, or
> whatever cheap unit you've got.
> 
> Why does a laptop AC adaptor improve this scenario?  Primarily, it is
> because the adapters have a large energy bucket inside, and are designed
> to produce a regulated DC output voltage under a wider range of power
> line conditions, to a heavier load.  They can produce 40 Watts, not 6W,
> and they can provide it over many missing power line cycles.  Think of
> it as a minature offline UPS.  If you use a 16V adapter, the Linksys
> internal regulator draws 6W/16V or about 0.4 amps during transmitting,
> and this means less power loss due to corrosion.   Empirically, using
> 16V laptop AC adaptors for powering Linksys devices JUST WORKS, with
> almost no unexplained resets compared to the marginally inadequate
> wall warts.  Again, read the web page I mention above.
> 
> 
> Computer people often have a hard time understanding soft margins. 
> It is assumed that something is either good or bad, without a gray
> area.  That is NOT how things work in the real world.  I promised
> to give a lecture to PLUG, "bits are a myth" (Real Soon Now), where
> I explain the analog nature of the "Real World" and why the digital
> approximation often fails.  Robust systems are designed with this
> in mind.  Robust software is designed to sense marginal conditions
> and recover quickly from small nondeterministic behavioral upsets.
> That means NEVER assuming that a sequence of correct instructions
> always produces the same result.  Always, always, test the result!
> 
> As we enter the era of unsteady power supply, and gigantic high speed
> systems pushed right to the margin of failure, system designers and
> programmers must take these effects into account or watch their
> products exhibit "mysterious" "flaky" behavior in the field.  There
> is actually nothing mysterious about the behavior; it is a fact of
> life.   The mystery is why programmers cling to the myth of complete
> mechanical determinism when experience contradicts it so often.
> 
> Keith
>

From bthoen at gisnet.com  Sun Jul  6 15:02:28 2008
From: bthoen at gisnet.com (Bill Thoen)
Date: Sun, 06 Jul 2008 16:02:28 -0600
Subject: [PLUG] FC9 Is SELinux Ready Yet?
Message-ID: <487140F4.2010602@gisnet.com>

I'm working with a database and mapping server I recently set up with 
Fedora Core 9, and it comes with SELinux set to "enforcing" mode by 
default.  The concept sounds pretty good but as I am a relative beginner 
to Linux system administration, I'm finding SELinux hard to understand 
and manage.  My question to those more experienced is, is this ready for 
prime time yet or is it still mostly experimental? Are there any dire 
consequences if I just set it to permissive mode so I won't have to 
spend all this time trying to figure how to write a security policy for 
everything I do it doesn't like?

Thanks for any advice,
- Bill Thoen


From alan at clueserver.org  Sun Jul  6 17:56:08 2008
From: alan at clueserver.org (Alan)
Date: Sun, 06 Jul 2008 17:56:08 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] FC9 Is SELinux Ready Yet?
In-Reply-To: <487140F4.2010602@gisnet.com>
References: <487140F4.2010602@gisnet.com>
Message-ID: <1215392168.5313.6.camel@localhost.localdomain>

On Sun, 2008-07-06 at 16:02 -0600, Bill Thoen wrote:
> I'm working with a database and mapping server I recently set up with 
> Fedora Core 9, and it comes with SELinux set to "enforcing" mode by 
> default.  The concept sounds pretty good but as I am a relative beginner 
> to Linux system administration, I'm finding SELinux hard to understand 
> and manage.  My question to those more experienced is, is this ready for 
> prime time yet or is it still mostly experimental? Are there any dire 
> consequences if I just set it to permissive mode so I won't have to 
> spend all this time trying to figure how to write a security policy for 
> everything I do it doesn't like?

Well, that depends on what you are doing.  For database use, it should
be fine.  There are tools that will show you what errors have occured
and where and give you an idea on how to resolve them.

Web servers are harder.  CGI code tends to do many complex things that
are hard to constrain.

Another pain is handling usb devices and other devices that can be
connected and disconnected.  Most work OK, but once in a while thay are
a pain.

There are some good books on SELinux.  The O'Reiely book was pretty
good.  If you are going to maintain a Redhat/Fedora system, you will
want to learn how it works.




From sjansen at buscaluz.org  Sun Jul  6 19:29:40 2008
From: sjansen at buscaluz.org (Stuart Jansen)
Date: Sun, 06 Jul 2008 20:29:40 -0600
Subject: [PLUG] FC9 Is SELinux Ready Yet?
In-Reply-To: <487140F4.2010602@gisnet.com>
References: <487140F4.2010602@gisnet.com>
Message-ID: <1215397780.9785.4.camel@simplicity.buscaluz.org>

On Sun, 2008-07-06 at 16:02 -0600, Bill Thoen wrote:
> My question to those more experienced is, is this ready for 
> prime time yet or is it still mostly experimental?

Fedora first shipped SELinux in FC3. With FC4 it was enabled by default.
Red Hat has shipped it with both RHEL4 and RHEL5. While SELinux
continues to improve at an impressive pace, it has been "ready for prime
time" for years. Unfortunately, education is lagging.

> Are there any dire 
> consequences if I just set it to permissive mode so I won't have to 
> spend all this time trying to figure how to write a security policy for 
> everything I do it doesn't like?

With SELinux in permissive mode, you're back to basic Unix permissions.
Not as secure as possible, but good enough for most people for decades.

On 7/7/08, Daniel Herrington <herda05 at gmail.com> wrote:
> All,
>
>  I just bought a Sun 420r and am attempting to put Solaris 10 on it. I'm
>  using a Startech SV125 convert so I can use a PS2 and PC Monitor.
>  However, I am not seeing an ok prompt after memory initialization. The
>  cursor just sits there. Nothing I type on the keyboard registers on the
>  screen. I'm assuming the startech isn't the problem since the monitor
>  connection is working correctly.
>
>  Does anybody know how the boot process on the Sun 420r works? I'm
>  assuming I should see an ok prompt at some point in which to tell the
>  server to use the cdrom as the boot device.

well, i'd say you should hit stop-a and it should take you back to ok>
but since you're not using a sun keyboard, i'm not sure how you'd do
that...

i agree with your analysis that if you see the memory analysis, you're
good on monitor connection.  have you tried plugging a serial cable in
to see if it's using the serial port as console?  i've never tried
doing that with a sun, so i don't know at what point the serial
console would take over from the framebuffer (or if it would at
all)...but i know it's possible to go completely headless, and still
get access to the prom prompt (and i'm pretty sure 'break' is the
equivalent of stop-a on a serial console).

some googling reveals that you'll want a nullmodem and 9600 8N1
settings for that serial connection.

From ianburrell at gmail.com  Mon Jul  7 16:06:43 2008
From: ianburrell at gmail.com (Ian Burrell)
Date: Mon, 7 Jul 2008 16:06:43 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] FC9 Is SELinux Ready Yet?
In-Reply-To: <487140F4.2010602@gisnet.com>
References: <487140F4.2010602@gisnet.com>
Message-ID: <d91f09cd0807071606y7520c35cyde5a55c896a39c69@mail.gmail.com>

On Sun, Jul 6, 2008 at 3:02 PM, Bill Thoen <bthoen at gisnet.com> wrote:
> I'm working with a database and mapping server I recently set up with
> Fedora Core 9, and it comes with SELinux set to "enforcing" mode by
> default.  The concept sounds pretty good but as I am a relative beginner
> to Linux system administration, I'm finding SELinux hard to understand
> and manage.  My question to those more experienced is, is this ready for
> prime time yet or is it still mostly experimental? Are there any dire
> consequences if I just set it to permissive mode so I won't have to
> spend all this time trying to figure how to write a security policy for
> everything I do it doesn't like?
>

I have found that the more stock the system, the easier SELinux is to
get working.  Third-party applications, especially services or
proprietary ones, that don't know about SELinux are a pain to get
working.  Complicated services, like web servers, where lots of files
need to be correctly labelled are annoying.  Red Hat has put in lots
of effort to improving SELinux so later releases of Fedora or RHEL are
better.

Permissive has two advantages over disabling SELinux completey.  One,
it keeps the audit logs  so you can fix problems.  The setroubleshoot
tools helps make this easier.  Two, it keeps the filesystem labelled
correctly so that you can enable SELinux without relabelling the whole
filesystem.

 - Ian

On Mon, 2008-07-07 at 15:58 -0700, chris (fool) mccraw wrote:
> On 7/7/08, Daniel Herrington <herda05 at gmail.com> wrote:
> > All,
> >
> >  I just bought a Sun 420r and am attempting to put Solaris 10 on it. I'm
> >  using a Startech SV125 convert so I can use a PS2 and PC Monitor.
> >  However, I am not seeing an ok prompt after memory initialization. The
> >  cursor just sits there. Nothing I type on the keyboard registers on the
> >  screen. I'm assuming the startech isn't the problem since the monitor
> >  connection is working correctly.
> >
> >  Does anybody know how the boot process on the Sun 420r works? I'm
> >  assuming I should see an ok prompt at some point in which to tell the
> >  server to use the cdrom as the boot device.
> 
> well, i'd say you should hit stop-a and it should take you back to ok>
> but since you're not using a sun keyboard, i'm not sure how you'd do
> that...

Break-A for a serial console. Usually serial console software like
minicom has a special key sequence you can use to send a break.

> i agree with your analysis that if you see the memory analysis, you're
> good on monitor connection.  have you tried plugging a serial cable in
> to see if it's using the serial port as console?  i've never tried
> doing that with a sun, so i don't know at what point the serial
> console would take over from the framebuffer (or if it would at
> all)...but i know it's possible to go completely headless, and still
> get access to the prom prompt (and i'm pretty sure 'break' is the
> equivalent of stop-a on a serial console).
> 
> some googling reveals that you'll want a nullmodem and 9600 8N1
> settings for that serial connection.

Suns are very easy to manage from a serial console, once you work out
the right serial incantation, but you don't get the framebuffer, because
that's graphical.

Might consider trying a USB keyboard too, if the system is of the
vintage that supports USB.

Wil

On Mon, 7 Jul 2008, Wil Cooley wrote:

> Suns are very easy to manage from a serial console, once you work 
> out the right serial incantation, but you don't get the framebuffer, 
> because that's graphical.

+1 on the recommendation to use the serial console -- unless of course 
you want this as an X workstation, in which case a graphical 
installation is probably a good idea. :-)

420R's are nice for mail or database servers. They tend to do well 
with I/O. Their CPUs aren't terribly speedy. They come in large 4U 
cases, IIRC: well designed, but heavy.

Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> www.madboa.com

From mhewan1 at comcast.net  Tue Jul  8 06:41:57 2008
From: mhewan1 at comcast.net (Michael Ewan)
Date: Tue, 08 Jul 2008 06:41:57 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Sun E420r help
In-Reply-To: <48729AC6.80805@gmail.com>
References: <48729AC6.80805@gmail.com>
Message-ID: <48736EA5.5010101@comcast.net>

Daniel Herrington wrote:
> All,
>
> I just bought a Sun 420r and am attempting to put Solaris 10 on it. I'm 
> using a Startech SV125 convert so I can use a PS2 and PC Monitor. 
> However, I am not seeing an ok prompt after memory initialization. The 
> cursor just sits there. Nothing I type on the keyboard registers on the 
> screen. I'm assuming the startech isn't the problem since the monitor 
> connection is working correctly.
>
> Does anybody know how the boot process on the Sun 420r works? I'm 
> assuming I should see an ok prompt at some point in which to tell the 
> server to use the cdrom as the boot device.
>
>   
If the Sun doesn't recognize the keyboard as a Sun type 5 then it will 
not display anything on the framebuffer, period.  The default console 
device is the serial port, if I remember correctly that unit has a DB25 
serial so you will need a DB25-DB9 null-modem cable.  As someone else 
said the Sun is very easy to manage from the serial port.  If you 
need/want the graphic console, you will need to find a real Sun type 5 
keyboard, that model did not support USB either.  Some of the PS2-Sun 
converters had a setting that would fake a type 5 so you might check the 
docs for the Startech, but you should be able to pick up a type 5 for a 
couple of bucks.

The 420r is a very nice unit, compact with four Ultra Sparc II 
processors and 4GB of RAM.  I think it had three internal drive bays for 
SCSI SCA 1" form factor disks.  If you need any Sun disk carriers, let 
me know.

# from Denis Heidtmann
# on Tuesday 08 July 2008 16:46:

>>>why does removing .asoundrc and .asoundrc.asoundconf 
>>>get things working?

I think it comes down to whatever was in those configs asking alsa to do 
the impossible.  I'm sure it was valid for your previous installation - 
and possibly generated by an older version of some config tool.  Major 
changes to device names (e.g. by the kernel, udev, etc), or possibly 
improvements in alsa which rendered an old work-around obsolete?  Or 
simply that the default configs (/usr/share/alsa/) renamed something -- 
the wiki says "Before ALSA 1.0.9 you often needed one to make things 
work at all ..."

>No, I had not. ?Thanks for the link. ?I just browsed it, and it makes
> me feel that deleting those files was a good idea. I note that
> /etc/asound.conf does not exist on my system either.
>
>There must be something in those files which tells ALSA to do the
> wrong things.

Yeah.  I think "the wrong things" were:

>>> ALSA lib confmisc.c:1286:(snd_func_refer) Unable to find definition 
>>> 'defaults.pcm.dmix.device'
>>> ...
>>> ALSA lib pcm.c:2145:(snd_pcm_open_noupdate) Unknown PCM default
>>> aplay: main:546: audio open error: No such file or directory

At least that sounds like the config was trying to point to a 
non-existent device or something like it being told that your preferred 
devices is the null string.

>>> This same collection of errors are produced if aplay is run as root.

For what value of "$HOME"?  It sounds like it was still loading the 
config from your user's $HOME. I wouldn't expect root's permissions to 
get past the "No such file or directory" -- root is all-powerful, but 
he can't make the software all-knowing ;-)

--Eric

"But as to modern architecture, let us drop it and let us take
modernistic out and shoot it at sunrise."
--F.L. Wright

>>  Now I'm stuck without being able to boot the cdrom.
>>
>>  I've tested the cd on linux, and I'm able to read it just fine, so I
>>  don't think the cd is bad (burnt using k3b on Ubuntu). The cdrom drive
>>  might be bad, and I can't think of a way to test it. The other thing is
>>  it might not be installed properly. I'm currently trolling through the
>>  service manual to figure out how to install the cdrom and make sure it's
>>  working. Thanks for the help so far.
>>     
>
> just for grins,
>
> from the prom,
>
> does 'show-devs' show the cdrom?
>
> also try 'diag-switch? true' for extended POST diags..
>   
That generation of machine had a SCSI cdrom, so just do

probe-scsi-all

to see if it shows up.  Make a note of where it is if it does show up, 
and check the definition of cdrom in the eeprom settings.  If I remember 
correctly, the eeprom command is 'showenv'.

You can also boot to the exact device using the whole device path.

Just a few quick thoughts that might help with debugging:

Have you looked at the output of:

  ifconfig <interface>

where <interface> is the name of your wireless interfaces to see how
many errors and what types of errors (rx/tx/etc) are being reported?

Have you tried pinging across the same path as your ssh session with
different sizes of packets?

I don't remember if you looked in your ssh server's log... does it give
any more details about what is going on?

good luck,
tim


From griffint at pobox.com  Wed Jul  9 13:57:20 2008
From: griffint at pobox.com (Terry Griffin)

Subject: [PLUG] SSH problem persists...
In-Reply-To: <20080709124309.c6de9uhgcmcog808@web.robinson-west.com>
References: <20080709124309.c6de9uhgcmcog808@web.robinson-west.com>
Message-ID: <54c9dc4e6b5c41c72129b24f0d9063b7.squirrel@webmail.whiz.to>

> Well, upgrading to 256 megs of ram didn't solve the problem.  At
> least now I can put a different version of Linux on.  I'm using
> ndiswrapper with a Realtek 8180 pci wireless card.  The RP-SMA
> connector on the card is loose and I can't tighten it down.
>
> I can ssh in, but I either have the session freeze or exit with
> bad packet length.  If I have to plug in via ethernet, that sort
> of defeats the purpose of the machine as a wireless repeater.  At
> least if I move the machine and switch to an ethernet connection,
> I'll be able to isolate the problem.  I'm looking at the prospect
> of burying an ethernet line and taking out the interbuilding
> wireless link.
>

This might be a long shot. But I just the other day I was having
flaky wireless behavior on a system on which I'd just replaced a hard
drive. It turned out to be that the SATA cable was too close to the
wireless card and was generating interference. When I rerouted the
cable it took care of the problem.

So check for noise sources both inside and near your box.

Terry

So this evening I decided to boot it up. All I can get is the splash
screen that says ASUS M2NPV-VM. It does not proceed to the Grub boot
menu. It does not give me an error message. It just hangs on the ASUS
splash screen.

There are two 320 GB hard drives in a software mirror with mdadm.

A Knoppix live CD boots just fine. During the boot process Knoppix says:

Scanning for hard disk partitions and creating /etc/fstab ... Done

After booting it sees sda1 through 4 and sdb1 through 4 (corresponding
to the four RAID partitions), but those are the partitions - the
filesystem is actually on mda2, which does not appear. 

Any suggestions for how I can repair it?

From johnxj at comcast.net  Wed Jul  9 22:53:04 2008
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jason Jordan)
Date: Wed, 9 Jul 2008 22:53:04 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] PGE killed my desktop
In-Reply-To: <487597D8.4000407@cesmail.net>
References: <20080709202414.fef7d285.johnxj@comcast.net>
	<487597D8.4000407@cesmail.net>
Message-ID: <20080709225304.c9f4e4e6.johnxj@comcast.net>

On Wed, 09 Jul 2008 22:02:16 -0700
"M. Edward (Ed) Borasky" <znmeb at cesmail.net> dijo:

> Does it boot a Hardy Heron LiveCD? I'd think that would have a better 
> shot at finding the partitions than Knoppix.

Thanks for the suggestion. It solved my problem, but not the way either
of us expected.

It did, indeed, boot a Hardy live CD. But when I checked in Nautilus it
did not find the hard drives either, same as Knoppix. 

However, it did find something else - a partition called "Windows
Backups," which it said it could not mount because the NTFS file system
was corrupt. Aha! At that point I remembered that I had added a 160 GB
USB drive that had at one time been used on a Windows system for
backups. In fact, I knew that the file system on the drive was bad and
had intended to reformat it to use for distro downloads. 

Acting on a hunch, I disconnected the USB drive and rebooted the
computer without a live CD. My old Hardy came up as usual and all is
back to normal.

My apologies to PGE. It was the flaky USB drive that was causing Ubuntu
not to boot. Having said that, a flaky external drive should not cause
the main filesystem not to load. Grub should go where it was told to go
and boot the OS it finds there.


From keithl at kl-ic.com  Wed Jul  9 23:36:11 2008
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Wed, 9 Jul 2008 23:36:11 -0700

In-Reply-To: <20080709202414.fef7d285.johnxj@comcast.net>

On Wed, Jul 09, 2008 at 08:24:14PM -0700, John Jason Jordan wrote:
...
> So this evening I decided to boot it up. All I can get is the splash
> screen that says ASUS M2NPV-VM. It does not proceed to the Grub boot
> menu. It does not give me an error message. It just hangs on the ASUS
> splash screen.
> 
> There are two 320 GB hard drives in a software mirror with mdadm.
> However, the hard drive light on the case does not come on.
> 
> A Knoppix live CD boots just fine. During the boot process Knoppix says:
> 
> Scanning for hard disk partitions and creating /etc/fstab ... Done
> 
> After booting it sees sda1 through 4 and sdb1 through 4 (corresponding
> to the four RAID partitions), but those are the partitions - the
> filesystem is actually on mda2, which does not appear. 
> 
> Any suggestions for how I can repair it?

Betcha a Penguino that the backup battery is worn out.  If you go
into setup mode (hold down DEL during boot) you may see some funny
settings.  If you made any changes from the default setup, I hope
you wrote them down someplace - then you can re-enter and save them,
and the machine will probably work. 

If it does, then the problem is likely the battery.   The battery
is a CR2032 3V lithium coin battery, on the left or bottom side,
depending on whether it is mounted horizontally or vertically ( I
run a M2NPV-VM too ).  A CR2025 will work, too, though it will be
a little loose and not last quite as long.

You can find the CR2032 almost anywhere; they are pretty standard
and should cost less than $4.  I will also bring one to the clinic.

Keith

On Thu, 10 Jul 2008 00:15:42 -0700
m0gely <m0gely at telestream.com> dijo:

> John Jason Jordan wrote:
> 
> > My apologies to PGE. It was the flaky USB drive that was causing Ubuntu
> > not to boot. Having said that, a flaky external drive should not cause
> > the main filesystem not to load. Grub should go where it was told to go
> > and boot the OS it finds there.
> 
> You said initially it never got to grub, so grub isn't guilty of 
> anything if it never got the chance to load. I'm betting the external 
> hard drive came up in the boot order before the internal drives.

When I read this my first reaction was "of course, why didn't I realize
it would try to boot from the USB device before the hard disk." But
then it occurred to me that I also had a flash drive inserted and it
didn't try to boot from it.

The BIOS is, indeed, set to boot first from the optical drive, second
from a USB drive, and *if no OS is found on either one*, then the hard
drive. So you are correct, Grub is not at fault. But the BIOS should
have found no OS on the USB drive and then proceeded to the hard drive.
The fault is in the BIOS.


From m0gely at telestream.com  Thu Jul 10 09:52:19 2008
From: m0gely at telestream.com (m0gely)
Date: Thu, 10 Jul 2008 09:52:19 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] PGE killed my desktop
In-Reply-To: <20080710092135.283be4c0.johnxj@comcast.net>
References: <20080709202414.fef7d285.johnxj@comcast.net>	<487597D8.4000407@cesmail.net>	<20080709225304.c9f4e4e6.johnxj@comcast.net>	<4875B71E.50401@telestream.com>
	<20080710092135.283be4c0.johnxj@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <48763E43.4070307@telestream.com>

John Jason Jordan wrote:

> The BIOS is, indeed, set to boot first from the optical drive, second
> from a USB drive, and *if no OS is found on either one*, then the hard
> drive. So you are correct, Grub is not at fault. But the BIOS should
> have found no OS on the USB drive and then proceeded to the hard drive.
> The fault is in the BIOS.

A drive doesn't have to have an OS on it to be bootable. It just needs a 
bootloader. Even if it's partially broken for whatever reason, if there 
is is a working MBR on the drive the computer goes to it. Once that 
happens, the search for additional booting drives stops. The hand off 
was made, the BIOS's job is over. Repartition the external drive and you 
should be good.

-- 
- m0gely



From keithl at kl-ic.com  Thu Jul 10 08:43:34 2008
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Thu, 10 Jul 2008 08:43:34 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Verizon FIOS - Traffic shaping against VOIP?
Message-ID: <20080710154334.GA14803@gate.kl-ic.com>


When looking at path delay between my Verizon FIOS home machine and
a machine in Portland on Alternet, I noticed some "interesting"
ping results.  1000 pings sent at 0.2sec intervals sent to the 
Verizon POP in Seattle revealed that the vast bulk of the pings
returned between 12 and 15 msec - about what one would expect from a
lightly loaded network.  However, about 50 (5%) of the pings returned
between 20msec and 80msec, with a fairly uniform distribution.  

This is not what I would expect;  I would instead expect that the
delays would follow a Poisson distribution, smoothly tapering towards
zero with longer delays.  However, I don't know a lot about network
traffic delays.  Is this normal, or are some of the packets being
intentionally delayed to screw up services like VOIP?

This weekend, I will run some longer ping trials to get a better
idea of the shape of the outliers, perhaps by running two pings
in parallel with slightly different intervals to see if (1) the
delays correlate and (2) they interfere because of some throttling
mechanism.  I will also run a looping ifconfig on the firewall to
do a total packet count on the interface.  Anything else I should
try,  such as setting particular QOS bits on the ping?

Also, it would be nice to try the ping tests against another 
Washington County Verizon FIOS customer.  Volunteers with an
IP address?

Keith

Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993

Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

Any large ISP is probably going to set the QoS priority on pings to be 
pretty low; which makes performance trending using only ping pretty 
difficult.

I would try hping using your protocol of choice (IP/UDP/ICMP) against 
the service ports you are trying to use.
Vary your packet size, etc to try and simulate normal usage.

Brent Jones

From alan at clueserver.org  Fri Jul 11 16:04:31 2008
From: alan at clueserver.org (Alan)
Date: Fri, 11 Jul 2008 16:04:31 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] ANNOUNCEMENT: PLUG Advanced Topics July 16th 2008
Message-ID: <1215817471.23060.17.camel@rotwang>

PLUG Advanced Topics

Date:      July 16th 2008
Time:      7pm - 9pm
Location:  Jax Bar
           826 SW 2nd Avenue
           Portland, OR

Topic:     Embedded Linux
Speaker:   Josh Triplett <josh at freedesktop.org>
           Portland State University

The talk will be on how to get started with embedded Linux, based
on my experiences with the flight computer for the Portland State
Aerospace Society.  My talk will walk through all the steps I followed
to get the flight computer booting up to a shell prompt, and explain
precisely what the system does between hitting the power button and
the shell prompt.  This will include a discussion of the u-boot
embedded bootloader, working with TFTP, handling onboard flash (MTD)
devices, working with a PowerPC device tree, using busybox, and
building an initramfs.

Usual meeting rules apply.

Alan> Topic: Embedded Linux Speaker: Josh Triplett
Alan> <josh at freedesktop.org> Portland State University

Alan> The talk will be on how to get started with embedded Linux,
Alan> based on my experiences with the flight computer for the
Alan> Portland State Aerospace Society.  My talk will walk through all
Alan> the steps I followed to get the flight computer booting up to a
Alan> shell prompt, and explain precisely what the system does between
Alan> hitting the power button and the shell prompt.  This will
Alan> include a discussion of the u-boot embedded bootloader, working
Alan> with TFTP, handling onboard flash (MTD) devices, working with a
Alan> PowerPC device tree, using busybox, and building an initramfs.

Hey, could someone video tape this for me?  I really wanted to go to
this, but got called out of town unexpectedly for work.  I hate
schedule conflicts!

Fedora 9 got some pretty unfavorable reviews for reasons like this. 
Still, there are "stable" distributions -- CentOS, Debian Etch, Gentoo 
stable, for example -- and there are "community / testing / unstable / 
bleeding edge" distros -- Fedora, openSUSE, Debian Lenny/Sid, Gentoo 
"~arch", Ubuntu, for example.

If you want stable, pick a stable distro and stick with it. You'll still 
probably get security updates every few days that you need to deal with, 
but it won't be 250 -- more like four or five at the most. It's like 
buying a car -- don't buy the first model year. :)

That said, I've had fairly good luck with openSUSE 11 on my laptop. It's 
the first Linux I've put on it that found and configured my Belkin USB 
wireless adapter, for example.

http://ruby-perspectives.blogspot.com/

From sjansen at buscaluz.org  Sat Jul 12 18:41:49 2008
From: sjansen at buscaluz.org (Stuart Jansen)
Date: Sat, 12 Jul 2008 19:41:49 -0600
Subject: [PLUG] What portion of Fedora is experimental?
In-Reply-To: <1215900551.19016.8.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>
References: <1215900551.19016.8.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>
Message-ID: <1215913309.7326.22.camel@simplicity.buscaluz.org>

On Sat, 2008-07-12 at 15:09 -0700, robinson-west user wrote:
> I am installing Fedora Core 9 on a new workstation.
> I noticed that the firefox is firefox 3 beta 5.

The blame for shipping a beta version of Firefox belongs more to
Mozilla's distro-unfriendly policies and unfortunate timing than to
Fedora.

> Internet speed is atrocious, 
> I'm trying to watch a flash movie that is about 
> Fedora Core 9.  Granted, my brother in the other 
> room is teleconferencing via Polycom PVX. ?Is 
> there some tuning trick I need to know about? I 
> suppose I should see if everything else is slow 
> too. 

Your bandwidth is being sucked up by another user. How is this Fedora's
fault? Or the site is slow. Again, not Fedora's fault.

> Fedora wants to install something like 250 
> updates, yuck.  How seriously do I need to take 
> these updates?  Why can't Linux distributors 
> update before they release?

Like all distros, Fedora spends months testing each new release, just
like you suggest. However, rather than remaining stuck in time for an
extended period of time, subjecting users to a sub-optimal experience by
only shipping critical security updates, Fedora has chosen a liberal
update policy.

Fedora's updates resolve both major and minor security problems, bugs
and developer curiosity. Fedora is meant to be a cutting edge
distribution for developers and enthusiasts. Its policies are designed
to minimize response time, keep things uncomplicated for unpaid
volunteers, and increase the exposure of cutting edge but (hopefully)
stable software.

I choose to use Fedora, Ubuntu and openSUSE because I want to be on the
cutting edge for several reasons. 1) I'm a developer and I prefer to
work with the latest technologies. 2) I'm a system administrator and I
need to know what's coming down the pipeline. 3) I can.

If you don't want to be on the cutting edge, use a "long term support"
distro like RHEL, CentOS, SLES or Debian Stable. (I'd also like to
suggest Ubuntu LTS, but it has had a pretty steady stream of updates
this time around and you apparently want to avoid that.)



From znmeb at cesmail.net  Sat Jul 12 19:03:39 2008
From: znmeb at cesmail.net (M. Edward (Ed) Borasky)

Stuart Jansen wrote:
> Like all distros, Fedora spends months testing each new release, just
> like you suggest. However, rather than remaining stuck in time for an
> extended period of time, subjecting users to a sub-optimal experience by
> only shipping critical security updates, Fedora has chosen a liberal
> update policy.

Still, rumor has it that Fedora 9 hasn't been as solid as Fedora 8 was 
with the same distance from the release. I don't have any hard metrics, 
just low-grade gossip and FUD from people who undoubtedly have axes to 
grind. I tested it on my laptop and probably would be running it today 
if openSUSE hadn't picked up my wireless. Fedora didn't.

> Fedora's updates resolve both major and minor security problems, bugs
> and developer curiosity. Fedora is meant to be a cutting edge
> distribution for developers and enthusiasts. Its policies are designed
> to minimize response time, keep things uncomplicated for unpaid
> volunteers, and increase the exposure of cutting edge but (hopefully)
> stable software.

Well ... I don't know how "unpaid" some of those volunteers are. For 
example, the maintainer of the Linux block layer, Jens Axboe, is paid by 
Oracle to maintain the Linux block layer. :) In any event, if you want 
the 2.6.25 kernel, you have to run Fedora 9, openSUSE 11, or some other 
distro in unstable/testing mode.

> I choose to use Fedora, Ubuntu and openSUSE because I want to be on the
> cutting edge for several reasons. 1) I'm a developer and I prefer to
> work with the latest technologies. 2) I'm a system administrator and I
> need to know what's coming down the pipeline. 3) I can.

Me too. In particular, the performance metric collection is better in 
2.6.25 than it was in 2.6.24. But some distros are easier to maintain on 
the edge than Fedora. Gentoo is the one I use, but Debian testing or 
unstable are also easier to work with than Fedora or openSUSE if you 
want cutting edge software.

The point is that you *can* run Debian and Gentoo in "stable" mode. I 
don't think you can do that with the "latest" Fedora or openSUSE or Ubuntu.

> If you don't want to be on the cutting edge, use a "long term support"
> distro like RHEL, CentOS, SLES or Debian Stable. (I'd also like to
> suggest Ubuntu LTS, but it has had a pretty steady stream of updates
> this time around and you apparently want to avoid that.)

RHEL and SLES are not free as in beer. CentOS or Debian stable are 
better choices for folks with limited budgets.

Well, I tried it on a vmware installation of freedos and it uninstalled
the packages it said it was updating instead of updating them.  The
algorithm should be:

     download

     verify download

     update

Where update might be uninstall old followed by install new.

If you can't get past steps 1 and 2, don't update.

     Michael C. Robinson

From danielmyoung at gmail.com  Tue Jul 15 11:39:55 2008
From: danielmyoung at gmail.com (Dan Young)
Date: Tue, 15 Jul 2008 11:39:55 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Checking source-port randomization of your caching DNS server
Message-ID: <2e42bd9b0807151139t256a1dfbnd6a0430ea24bec7c@mail.gmail.com>

Assuming everybody's heard about the impending DNS doom by now:
http://www.kb.cert.org/vuls/id/800113

Looking over our own systems, I found a tool to check the source port
randomization of your DNS resolver:
https://www.dns-oarc.net/oarc/services/porttest

Basically:
dig +short porttest.dns-oarc.net TXT
z.y.x.w.v.u.t.s.r.q.p.o.n.m.l.k.j.i.h.g.f.e.d.c.b.a.pt.dns-oarc.net.
"<your nameserver ip> is GOOD: 26 queries in 0.5 seconds from 26 ports
with std dev 19481.67"

Some of our nameservers had explicitly set the source port in
named.conf w/ "query-source address * port 53;" so these were not
randomizing the source-port even after updating BIND. Commented those
out, of course. Probably worth checking your DNS servers even if
you've applied your distro's updates.

Cheers,

-- 
Dan Young

In the meantime, I've installed Gramps which looks like it will
do if it can import data from Family Tree.  I'm having a hard 
time selling Gramps to my dad.  It looks like a capable 
substitute for Family Tree 2008 and it is free.  I don't know 
if my dad's numerous Gedcom files will import, but that's another
matter.  I for one think that open source genealogy software 
makes a lot of sense.  Why trust family data and vital statistics 
to something that is closed where the platform it runs on is also 
closed and losing support?  My dad is complaining that Gramps 
looks stripped down compared to Family Tree.  Stripped down???
Is Gramps the only open source genealogy software worth looking
into?  Has anyone migrated from Family Tree to Gramps that can
vouch for it?


        Michael C. Robinson



From donkyhotay at verizon.net  Wed Jul 16 06:55:41 2008
From: donkyhotay at verizon.net (donkyhotay)
Date: Wed, 16 Jul 2008 06:55:41 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Gramps
In-Reply-To: <1216185437.1393.44.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>
References: <1216185437.1393.44.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>
Message-ID: <487DFDDD.8050600@verizon.net>

I made the transition but it was from Family Tree 5.0 which was released 
in the win95 days so that needs to be taken into consideration. The 
gedcom files transferred over with very little issue. Compared to Family 
Tree 5.0, Gramps is quite an advanced program that works very well. It 
is also the only open source genealogy program I've ever found. If there 
is another one I'd like to know myself because I'm occasionally asked 
about free genealogy programs for use on windows based systems and 
gramps of course doesn't work on windows. Sure the people asking me are 
asking for free (beer) software but I like to refer people to a free 
(speech) software if I can as a way to introduce the concept and 
capabilities of open source software.

On Wed, Jul 16, 2008 at 6:55 AM, donkyhotay <donkyhotay at verizon.net> wrote:
> I made the transition but it was from Family Tree 5.0 which was released
> in the win95 days so that needs to be taken into consideration. The
> gedcom files transferred over with very little issue. Compared to Family
> Tree 5.0, Gramps is quite an advanced program that works very well. It
> is also the only open source genealogy program I've ever found. If there
> is another one I'd like to know myself because I'm occasionally asked
> about free genealogy programs for use on windows based systems and
> gramps of course doesn't work on windows. Sure the people asking me are
> asking for free (beer) software but I like to refer people to a free
> (speech) software if I can as a way to introduce the concept and
> capabilities of open source software.
>
>
> robinson-west user wrote:
>> In the meantime, I've installed Gramps which looks like it will
>> do if it can import data from Family Tree.  I'm having a hard
>> time selling Gramps to my dad.  It looks like a capable
>> substitute for Family Tree 2008 and it is free.  I don't know
>> if my dad's numerous Gedcom files will import, but that's another
>> matter.  I for one think that open source genealogy software
>> makes a lot of sense.  Why trust family data and vital statistics
>> to something that is closed where the platform it runs on is also
>> closed and losing support?  My dad is complaining that Gramps
>> looks stripped down compared to Family Tree.  Stripped down???
>> Is Gramps the only open source genealogy software worth looking
>> into?  Has anyone migrated from Family Tree to Gramps that can
>> vouch for it?
>>
>>

Well, not free speech but free from cost; use familysearch.org as the
starting point and their software: http://tinyurl.com/6en9jt (
couldn't stand pasting a 530 char url into email).

If you want a web-based project check out PhpGedView (
http://phpgedview.sf.net). I've been using it for years personally and it
has worked well. Being web-based it makes collaboration with family and
friends easier.

From iconoklastic at yahoo.com  Wed Jul 16 20:42:46 2008
From: iconoklastic at yahoo.com (Robert Kopp)
Date: Wed, 16 Jul 2008 20:42:46 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Youtube upload problem
Message-ID: <374853.75869.qm@web50606.mail.re2.yahoo.com>

I can make video clips in .mp4 format with VLC in Linux and upload them to Youtube. When I do so, however, the image looks somewhat blurred:

http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=oKRSjlTdraE

However, when Windows Movie Maker is used to make video clips, and they are uploaded in .wmv format, they look as they should. If anyone has used Linux applications to make Youtube videos, with satisfactory results, I would appreciate some advice. I like VLC, so if it can be used for that purpose, I might as well stay with it.

 Robert "Tim" Kopp 
http://analytic.tripod.com/


From iconoklastic at yahoo.com  Wed Jul 16 21:43:31 2008
From: iconoklastic at yahoo.com (Robert Kopp)
Date: Wed, 16 Jul 2008 21:43:31 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Youtube upload problem
Message-ID: <527431.82035.qm@web50610.mail.re2.yahoo.com>

Solution: use ffmpeg to convert the file to .flv before uploading it to Youtube.

http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=uTmFas7mOz8

 Robert "Tim" Kopp 
http://analytic.tripod.com/

From merlyn at stonehenge.com  Thu Jul 17 10:15:57 2008
From: merlyn at stonehenge.com (Randal L. Schwartz)
Date: Thu, 17 Jul 2008 10:15:57 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] PCM/Cable Access show for Linux or FOSS?
References: <487F564D.9080109@f2it.com>
Message-ID: <867ibkbfea.fsf@blue.stonehenge.com>

>>>>> "Jay" == Jay D Allen <jay at f2it.com> writes:

Jay>     Does anyone know if there is any current Cable Access TV about 
Jay> Linux, or Open Source?  I looked at the online schedule on 
Jay> http://www.pcmtv.org (Portland Cable Access), but I only see a very few, 
Jay> very old shows listed.

Given the prevalence of cheap hosting and cheap or free live video streaming,
is cable access even relevant any more?

I mean, if you want content delivered on a regular basis about FLOSS,
subscribe to my FLOSS Weekly podcast (twit.tv/floss), and there are a dozen
Linux podcasts as well.

If you want to create a video stream, you can push HD content for free to
vimeo.com (500mb per week!), or go live with mogulus.com or stickam.com or
ustream.com or video.yahoo.com, etc etc.  If you want a phone-in talk show
that later becomes a podcast, talkshoe.com is also free.

I say this as a heavy cable access producer back in the early 90s.  I was
chairman of the advisory board of TVTV, and could find my name in credits
about 15 times a week.

But new media has taken over.  With the advent of live free video streaming
and cheap downloads and hosting, and inexpensive audio and video capture
hardware and editing software, cable access is now a dinosaur.

--

<merlyn at stonehenge.com> <URL:http://www.stonehenge.com/merlyn/>

From jay at f2it.com  Thu Jul 17 10:47:48 2008

Hey Randal,

    I agree with you on two points.  1) Lots of new media outlets/inlets
2) Cable access is less relevant.  I guess I would suggest that it
still brings some value to the community.  For example, the media
education programs.

    Ten years ago, the local Cable Access was focused on technical
enablement; How to use cameras, and sound equipment, etc.  Now the
technical barrier to entry has dropped so low, that kind of focus does
not make sense.  Thats why you see classes on story-telling and
framing the message.  Cable Access is still enabling, they are just
moving up the value chain.

    Also, as hard as it is to believe, some people still watch TV, on the
TV, so new media outlets are missing that audience.

Jay D Allen
http://www.f2it.com

I've had pretty good results with the Zoom H2 portable audio recorder (about
$180 from amazon) as a field recorder, using the stereo recording in
front/back mode to get a 360 degree coverage.  It has a fairly usable
auto-gain mode that will "gain up" when the audience is asking a question, and
"gain down" when the speaker is speaking.

The Zoom H2 can also work "live" on a USB (even being powered by the USB), so
it works well as a desk or voice-over mike, or even as the pickup mike for a
live stream.

I'm in town for the Aug 7 PLUG meeting.  I'll bring my gear (laptop and H2),
and we can try a live feed.  I've just confirmed that talkshoe.com can
broadcast and record a show that is greater than two hours.  Before Aug 7,
remote listeners should visit talkshoe.com and make sure your particular setup
is compatible.  Since feeds can have only one owner (sadly), I'll create a
PLUG "owner" which we can pass around when different people are mastering it.

As a cool part, the talkshoe listeners have a chat room, which we can use to
pass questions on to the speaker.  In fact, I might even be able to pull the
audio out into the room, but that may feedback badly.

*After* the meeting is over, the recording will go into a podcast feed (RSS
with embedded media) on talkshoe.com, which we can then publish as part of the
official PLUG archive.  The *chat* will not be recorded, however.  Sorry.

In parallel, if I have enough bandwidth (and screenspace, and stamina), I
might also be able to link up a video that would go live out on stickam or
live.yahoo.com.  Again, that may or may not be recorded and/or archived, but
if it is archived, I can host it up on vimeo.com in a PLUG group that anyone
else can also contribute to.

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Sat Jul 19 08:54:32 2008
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Sat, 19 Jul 2008 08:54:32 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Somewhat random failures
In-Reply-To: <8528.12.172.32.236.1216420058.squirrel@clueserver.org>
References: <48811755.8020404@dslnorthwest.net>
	<8528.12.172.32.236.1216420058.squirrel@clueserver.org>
Message-ID: <20080719155432.GB27098@gate.kl-ic.com>

On Fri, Jul 18, 2008 at 03:27:38PM -0700, Alan wrote:

> Have you run memtest86 to check for memory problems?
...
> If that is not the problem, you may have a dying motherboard.

Does anyone have a good evaluation of memtest86 as a stress test? 
It seems like memtest86 subjects the system to a high but steady
load, and there are a lot of failures that don't show up unless
the system is thrashing.  Denis is running rsync/dirvish, and
that is a more realistic stress test.  Denis, does the system
ever fail during backups?

As Alan suggests, memory does wear out - or more precisely,
individual bits drift from "marginal good" to "marginal bad". 
While I would expect a different set of errors than CMOS and
clock errors,  I will bow to his experience here.  At least
bad memory is easier to replace than a whole motherboard, and
it is best to check hardware in "frequency of failure" and
"ease of replacement" order. 


I have seen marginal behavior when a fan goes out.  Sometimes
it is merely a matter of adding or replacing fans;  a cooler
machine almost always runs better than a hot one. 

A common failure mode in older motherboards is the electrolytic
capacitors drying out.  The manufacturers save often save a few
pennies by using fewer and cheaper capacitors than the "Intel
reference design" specifies.  The motherboard still works, for
a while, but drops below minimum margins faster.  There are a
lot of places to shave pennies at the expense of longevity, and
unless your machine is from a high-end manufacturer, one or the
other of these shortcuts may eventually lead to flakiness, and
then failure.   

In the newest, fastest machines, the processors are running so
much current that it degrades the power supply pins on the
processor socket, slowly increasing resistance and inductance
until the internal power spikes cause failures when the processor
"gear shifts" from fast to slow or back.

Sadly, there is no place to go to find good engineering evaluations
of these products.  There are also no good hardware tools to
accurately diagnose and repair items like PC motherboards.  I would
love to have a PCI board that monitors the bus, looking for voltage
spikes and noisy waveforms and other trouble sources.  Of course,
what it would probably end up telling you is that everything is
marginal crap.  Sigh.

Keith

-- 
Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993
KLIC --- Keith Lofstrom Integrated Circuits --- "Your Ideas in Silicon"
Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

Keith Lofstrom wrote:
> On Fri, Jul 18, 2008 at 03:27:38PM -0700, Alan wrote:
> 
>> Have you run memtest86 to check for memory problems?
> ...
>> If that is not the problem, you may have a dying motherboard.
> 
> Does anyone have a good evaluation of memtest86 as a stress test? 
> It seems like memtest86 subjects the system to a high but steady
> load, and there are a lot of failures that don't show up unless
> the system is thrashing.  Denis is running rsync/dirvish, and
> that is a more realistic stress test.  Denis, does the system
> ever fail during backups?
> 
> As Alan suggests, memory does wear out - or more precisely,
> individual bits drift from "marginal good" to "marginal bad". 
> While I would expect a different set of errors than CMOS and
> clock errors,  I will bow to his experience here.  At least
> bad memory is easier to replace than a whole motherboard, and
> it is best to check hardware in "frequency of failure" and
> "ease of replacement" order. 
> 
> 
> I have seen marginal behavior when a fan goes out.  Sometimes
> it is merely a matter of adding or replacing fans;  a cooler
> machine almost always runs better than a hot one. 
> 
> A common failure mode in older motherboards is the electrolytic
> capacitors drying out.  The manufacturers save often save a few
> pennies by using fewer and cheaper capacitors than the "Intel
> reference design" specifies.  The motherboard still works, for
> a while, but drops below minimum margins faster.  There are a
> lot of places to shave pennies at the expense of longevity, and
> unless your machine is from a high-end manufacturer, one or the
> other of these shortcuts may eventually lead to flakiness, and
> then failure.   
> 
> In the newest, fastest machines, the processors are running so
> much current that it degrades the power supply pins on the
> processor socket, slowly increasing resistance and inductance
> until the internal power spikes cause failures when the processor
> "gear shifts" from fast to slow or back.
> 
> Sadly, there is no place to go to find good engineering evaluations
> of these products.  There are also no good hardware tools to
> accurately diagnose and repair items like PC motherboards.  I would
> love to have a PCI board that monitors the bus, looking for voltage
> spikes and noisy waveforms and other trouble sources.  Of course,
> what it would probably end up telling you is that everything is
> marginal crap.  Sigh.
> 
> Keith
> 
I appreciate the sad state that capitalism has brought the high-tech industry, 
but what might be the best course of action now that my system passed the memory 
tests? (3 passes w/76k reserved memory, 2 passes w/0 reserved memory.)  I can 
run the memory test overnight,  What else?  Remove and reseat connectors? 
Replace the power supply?  If replacing the MB moves to the top of the list, 
what choices should I consider? (Presently I have 1.5G Pentium 4.  Performance 
is satisfactory.)

-Denis


From znmeb at cesmail.net  Sat Jul 19 14:23:29 2008
From: znmeb at cesmail.net (znmeb at cesmail.net)
Date: Sat, 19 Jul 2008 17:23:29 -0400

Quoting Russell Senior <russell at personaltelco.net>:

>
> I am looking for existing FOSS software that can take a description of
> a network and convert it into a nice graphic.  I know I can use
> various tools to draw my own network diagram, but I'm looking for
> something that works from a "source" file rather than lots and lots of
> little mouse clicks and drags and stuff.
>
> Any suggestions?

I think the easiest thing would be to load dia, make a sample diagram  
with one of every gizmo you've got on your network, then save it as an  
XML file. Then you can "just" open it up in an XML editor and cut and  
paste away.

If this is *your* network, you might be able to find some "network  
discovery" tools that would automate a bit more of the process. But if  
you're actually looking to code something, you'd probably find it  
easier to use dia to get you started.

Dia is scriptable, but I don't remember what the scripting language  
is. It's either Python or Guile, which is a Scheme dialect.
>
>

From scratchcomputing at gmail.com  Sat Jul 19 15:40:27 2008
From: scratchcomputing at gmail.com (Eric Wilhelm)

Subject: [PLUG] Somewhat random failures

	<20080719155432.GB27098@gate.kl-ic.com>

Message-ID: <200807191540.27312.ewilhelm@cpan.org>

# from Denis Heidtmann
# on Saturday 19 July 2008 14:32:

>Remove and reseat connectors?

It is related to PCI, so perhaps check the startup messages for 
something to that effect and also run `lspci` when you're in a state 
of "sound card missing".

If that is your only pci card, finding something (spare NIC?) to plug-in 
next to it would help you determine whether you're suffering a bad 
connection at the card or a problem with pci on the motherboard.  (If 
you have two pci cards plugged in and lspci shows one but not the sound 
card, that points at the sound card (or at least that slot on the MB.))

I don't know what the output of lspci would look like if the pci bus 
were MIA.

So, you'll need to try to reliably replicate the "missing sound card"  
state - does reboot vs power cycle vs cold boot make a difference?  If 
it is too frustrating to replicate, replacing the motherboard and/or 
sound card might be more fun.

--Eric

From scratchcomputing at gmail.com  Sat Jul 19 15:50:02 2008
From: scratchcomputing at gmail.com (Eric Wilhelm)
Date: Sat, 19 Jul 2008 15:50:02 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] software for automatic network diagrams?
In-Reply-To: <867ibhd2fl.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>
References: <867ibhd2fl.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>
Message-ID: <200807191550.02560.ewilhelm@cpan.org>

# from Russell Senior
# on Saturday 19 July 2008 13:49:

> but I'm looking for
>something that works from a "source" file rather than lots and lots of
>little mouse clicks and drags and stuff.

Graphviz (aka dot).

  digraph "my network" {
    node1 -> switch1;
    node2 -> switch1;
    switch1 -> switch2;
    node3 -> switch2;
    node4 -> switch2;
  }

It then does the node layout automatically.  You can group things with a 
subgraph{} and influence the placement by ordering or add explicit 
placement by coordinates.

It may not be well-suited to creating small tidy diagrams, but it works 
great for mapping large quantities of connections.

--Eric
-- 
"I've often gotten the feeling that the only people who have learned
from computer assisted instruction are the authors."
--Ben Schneiderman

    http://scratchcomputing.com

From russell at personaltelco.net  Sat Jul 19 22:53:20 2008
From: russell at personaltelco.net (Russell Senior)
